<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Fraction</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Ratio of two numbers</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Fraction_(disambiguation)" title="Fraction (disambiguation)">Fraction (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cake_quarters.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="383" data-file-width="504" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Cake_quarters.svg/220px-Cake_quarters.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Cake_quarters.svg/330px-Cake_quarters.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Cake_quarters.svg/440px-Cake_quarters.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cake_quarters.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A cake with one quarter (one fourth) removed. The remaining three fourths are shown by dotted lines and labeled by the <span class="nowrap">fraction <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1050945101">.mw-parser-output .sfrac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .sfrac.tion,.mw-parser-output .sfrac .tion{display:inline-block;vertical-align:-0.5em;font-size:85%;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .sfrac .num,.mw-parser-output .sfrac .den{display:block;line-height:1em;margin:0 0.1em}.mw-parser-output .sfrac .den{border-top:1px solid}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">4</span></span></span></div></div></div>
<p>A <b>fraction</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">fractus</i>, "broken") represents a part of a whole or, more generally, any number of equal parts. When spoken in everyday English, a fraction describes how many parts of a certain size there are, for example, one-half, eight-fifths, three-quarters. A <i>common</i>, <i>vulgar</i>, or <i>simple</i> fraction (examples: <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{2}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{2}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {1}{2}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/edef8290613648790a8ac1a95c2fb7c3972aea2f" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:1.658ex; height:3.509ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {17}{3}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>17</mn>
<mn>3</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {17}{3}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {17}{3}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8af3ddb2b7911535514c1999a286cfc7a4aa5272" style="vertical-align: -1.338ex; width:2.48ex; height:3.843ex;"/></span>) consists of a <b>numerator</b>, displayed above a line (or before a slash like <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">2</span></span>), and a non-zero <b>denominator</b>, displayed below (or after) that line. Numerators and denominators are also used in fractions that are not <i>common</i>, including compound fractions, complex fractions, and mixed numerals.
</p><p>In positive common fractions, the numerator and denominator are <a href="/wiki/Natural_number" title="Natural number">natural numbers</a>. The numerator represents a number of equal parts, and the denominator indicates how many of those parts make up a unit or a whole. The denominator cannot be zero, because zero parts can never make up a whole. For example, in the fraction <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">3</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">4</span></span>, the numerator 3 indicates that the fraction represents 3 equal parts, and the denominator 4 indicates that 4 parts make up a whole. The picture to the right illustrates <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">3</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">4</span></span> of a cake.
</p><p>A common fraction is a numeral which represents a <a href="/wiki/Rational_number" title="Rational number">rational number</a>. That same number can also be represented as a <a href="/wiki/Decimal" title="Decimal">decimal</a>, a percent, or with a negative exponent. For example, 0.01, 1%, and 10<sup>−2</sup> are all equal to the fraction 1/100. An <a href="/wiki/Integer" title="Integer">integer</a> can be thought of as having an implicit denominator of one (for example, 7 equals 7/1).
</p><p>Other uses for fractions are to represent <a href="/wiki/Ratio" title="Ratio">ratios</a> and <a href="/wiki/Division_(mathematics)" title="Division (mathematics)">division</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Thus the fraction <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">3</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">4</span></span> can also be used to represent the ratio 3:4 (the ratio of the part to the whole), and the division <span class="nowrap">3 ÷ 4</span> (three divided by four). The non-zero denominator rule, which applies when representing a division as a fraction, is an example of the rule that <a href="/wiki/Division_by_zero" title="Division by zero">division by zero</a> is undefined.
</p><p>We can also write negative fractions, which represent the opposite of a positive fraction. For example, if <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span> represents a half-dollar profit, then −<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span> represents a half-dollar loss. Because of the rules of division of signed numbers (which states in part that negative divided by positive is negative), −<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span>, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">−1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span> and <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">−2</span></span> all represent the same fraction –  negative one-half. And because a negative divided by a negative produces a positive, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">−1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">−2</span></span> represents positive one-half.
</p><p>In mathematics the set of all numbers that can be expressed in the form <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><i>a</i></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den"><i>b</i></span></span>, where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are integers and <i>b</i> is not zero, is called the set of rational numbers and is represented by the symbol <b>Q</b>, which stands for <a href="/wiki/Quotient" title="Quotient">quotient</a>. A number is a rational number precisely when it can be written in that form (i.e., as a common fraction). However, the word <i>fraction</i> can also be used to describe mathematical expressions that are not rational numbers. Examples of these usages include <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_fraction" title="Algebraic fraction">algebraic fractions</a> (quotients of algebraic expressions), and expressions that contain <a href="/wiki/Irrational_number" title="Irrational number">irrational numbers</a>, such as <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\textstyle {\frac {\sqrt {2}}{2}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<msqrt>
<mn>2</mn>
</msqrt>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\textstyle {\frac {\sqrt {2}}{2}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\textstyle {\frac {\sqrt {2}}{2}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3b4ed07b85acbae37e000a62a14bdb10f70d7d4a" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:3.027ex; height:4.176ex;"/></span> (see <a href="/wiki/Square_root_of_2" title="Square root of 2">square root of 2</a>) and <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">π</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">4</span></span> (see <a href="/wiki/Proof_that_%CF%80_is_irrational" title="Proof that π is irrational">proof that π is irrational</a>).
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Fractions_in_abstract_mathematics"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Fractions in abstract mathematics</span></a></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">Vocabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fraction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Numeral_(linguistics)#Fractional_numbers" title="Numeral (linguistics)">Numeral (linguistics) § Fractional numbers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/English_numerals#Fractions_and_decimals" title="English numerals">English numerals § Fractions and decimals</a></div>
<p>In a fraction, the number of equal parts being described is the <b>numerator</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">numerātor</i>, "counter" or "numberer"), and the type or variety of the parts is the <b>denominator</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">dēnōminātor</i>, "thing that names or designates").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schwartzman_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-schwartzman-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> As an example, the fraction <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">8</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">5</span></span> amounts to eight parts, each of which is of the type named "fifth". In terms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Division_(math)" title="Division (math)">division</a>, the numerator corresponds to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Division_(math)" title="Division (math)">dividend</a>, and the denominator corresponds to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Division_(math)" title="Division (math)">divisor</a>.
</p><p>Informally, the numerator and denominator may be distinguished by placement alone, but in formal contexts they are usually separated by a <b>fraction bar</b>. The fraction bar may be horizontal (as in <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">3</span></span>), oblique (as in 2/5), or diagonal (as in <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">4</span>⁄<span class="den">9</span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ambrose_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-ambrose-4">[4]</a></sup> These marks are respectively known as the horizontal bar; the virgule, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slash_mark" title="Slash mark">slash</a> (<a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">US</a>), or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oblique_stroke" title="Oblique stroke">stroke</a> (<a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">UK</a>); and the fraction bar, solidus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fraction_slash" title="Fraction slash">fraction slash</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[n 1]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Typography" title="Typography">typography</a>, fractions stacked vertically are also known as "<a href="/wiki/En_(typography)" title="En (typography)">en</a>" or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/En_dash" title="En dash">nut</a> fractions", and diagonal ones as "<a href="/wiki/Em_(typography)" title="Em (typography)">em</a>" or "mutton fractions", based on whether a fraction with a single-digit numerator and denominator occupies the proportion of a narrow <i>en</i> square, or a wider <i>em</i> square.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ambrose_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-ambrose-4">[4]</a></sup> In traditional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Typefounding" title="Typefounding">typefounding</a>, a piece of type bearing a complete fraction (e.g. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span>) was known as a "case fraction", while those representing only part of fraction were called "piece fractions".
</p><p>The denominators of English fractions are generally expressed as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordinal_number_(linguistics)#Variations" title="Ordinal number (linguistics)">ordinal numbers</a>, in the plural if the numerator is not 1. (For example, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">2</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">5</span></span> and <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">3</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">5</span></span> are both read as a number of "fifths".) Exceptions include the denominator 2, which is always read "half" or "halves", the denominator 4, which may be alternatively expressed as "quarter"/"quarters" or as "fourth"/"fourths", and the denominator 100, which may be alternatively expressed as "hundredth"/"hundredths" or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Percent" title="Percent">percent</a>". 
</p><p>When the denominator is 1, it may be expressed in terms of "wholes" but is more commonly ignored, with the numerator read out as a whole number. For example, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">3</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">1</span></span> may be described as "three wholes", or simply as "three". When the numerator is 1, it may be omitted (as in "a tenth" or "each quarter").
</p><p>The entire fraction may be expressed as a single composition, in which case it is hyphenated, or as a number of fractions with a numerator of one, in which case they are not. (For example, "two-fifths" is the fraction <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">2</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">5</span></span> and "two fifths" is the same fraction understood as 2 instances of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">5</span></span>.) Fractions should always be hyphenated when used as adjectives. Alternatively, a fraction may be described by reading it out as the numerator "over" the denominator, with the denominator expressed as a <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_number" title="Cardinal number">cardinal number</a>. (For example, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">3</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">1</span></span> may also be expressed as "three over one".) The term "over" is used even in the case of solidus fractions, where the numbers are placed left and right of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slash_mark" title="Slash mark">slash mark</a>. (For example, 1/2 may be read "one-half", "one half", or "one over two".) Fractions with large denominators that are <i>not</i> powers of ten are often rendered in this fashion (e.g., <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">117</span></span> as "one over one hundred seventeen"), while those with denominators divisible by ten are typically read in the normal ordinal fashion (e.g., <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">6</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">1000000</span></span> as "six-millionths", "six millionths", or "six one-millionths").
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Forms_of_fractions">Forms of fractions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fraction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Forms of fractions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Simple.2C_common.2C_or_vulgar_fractions"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Simple,_common,_or_vulgar_fractions">Simple, common, or vulgar fractions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fraction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Simple, common, or vulgar fractions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A <b>simple fraction</b> (also known as a <b>common fraction</b> or <b>vulgar fraction</b>, where vulgar is Latin for "common") is a <a href="/wiki/Rational_number" title="Rational number">rational number</a> written as <i>a</i>/<i>b</i> or <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {a}{b}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mi>a</mi>
<mi>b</mi>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {a}{b}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {a}{b}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/67e9c32a14514b5b975a4666af015884bc93b0b8" style="vertical-align: -1.338ex; width:1.706ex; height:3.343ex;"/></span>, where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Integers" title="Integers">integers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> As with other fractions, the denominator (<i>b</i>) cannot be zero. Examples include <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{2}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {8}{-5}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7d221dc9e5e9041fc176d8ea88ecf71749747266" style="vertical-align: -1.338ex; width:2.937ex; height:3.676ex;"/></span>. The term was originally used to distinguish this type of fraction from the <a href="/wiki/Sexagesimal#Fractions" title="Sexagesimal">sexagesimal fraction</a> used in astronomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith1958_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith1958-11">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Common fractions</i> can be positive or negative, and they can be proper or improper (see below). Compound fractions, complex fractions, mixed numerals, and decimals (see below) are not <i>common fractions;</i> though, unless irrational, they can be evaluated to a common fraction.
</p>
<ul><li>A <a href="/wiki/Unit_fraction" title="Unit fraction">unit fraction</a> is a common fraction with a numerator of 1 (e.g., <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{7}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>7</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{7}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {1}{7}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/afcab3a9c6842020657bf281789284c1a05b1ff8" style="vertical-align: -1.338ex; width:1.658ex; height:3.676ex;"/></span>). Unit fractions can also be expressed using negative exponents, as in 2<sup>−1</sup>, which represents 1/2, and 2<sup>−2</sup>, which represents 1/(2<sup>2</sup>) or 1/4.</li>
<li>A <a href="/wiki/Dyadic_rational" title="Dyadic rational">dyadic fraction</a> is a common fraction in which the denominator is a <a href="/wiki/Power_of_two" title="Power of two">power of two</a>, e.g. <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{8}}={\tfrac {1}{2^{3}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<msup>
<mn>2</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>3</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{8}}={\tfrac {1}{2^{3}}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{8}}={\tfrac {1}{2^{3}}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/27b4512cf1aeca3dca95cd841b086016d58a3af1" style="vertical-align: -1.671ex; width:7.246ex; height:4.009ex;"/></span>.</li></ul>
<p>In Unicode, precomposed fraction characters are in the <a href="/wiki/Number_Forms" title="Number Forms">Number Forms</a> block.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proper_and_improper_fractions">Proper and improper fractions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fraction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Proper and improper fractions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Common fractions can be classified as either proper or improper. When the numerator and the denominator are both positive, the fraction is called proper if the numerator is less than the denominator, and improper otherwise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> The concept of an "improper fraction" is a late development, with the terminology deriving from the fact that "fraction" means "a piece", so a proper fraction must be less than 1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith1958_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smith1958-11">[10]</a></sup> This was explained in the 17th century textbook <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ground_of_Arts" title="The Ground of Arts">The Ground of Arts</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams2011_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams2011-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Record1654_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Record1654-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>In general, a common fraction is said to be a <b>proper fraction</b>, if the <a href="/wiki/Absolute_value" title="Absolute value">absolute value</a> of the fraction is strictly less than one—that is, if the fraction is greater than −1 and less than 1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> It is said to be an <b>improper fraction</b>, or sometimes <b>top-heavy fraction</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> if the absolute value of the fraction is greater than or equal to 1. Examples of proper fractions are 2/3, −3/4, and 4/9, whereas examples of improper fractions are 9/4, −4/3, and 3/3.
</p>
<h3><span id="Reciprocals_and_the_.22invisible_denominator.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='Reciprocals_and_the_"invisible_denominator"'>Reciprocals and the "invisible denominator"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fraction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title='Edit section: Reciprocals and the "invisible denominator"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <b>reciprocal</b> of a fraction is another fraction with the numerator and denominator exchanged. The reciprocal of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {3}{7}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>3</mn>
<mn>7</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {3}{7}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {3}{7}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/15f2b824decf224d9a3143d4271666c7fba7ac83" style="vertical-align: -1.338ex; width:1.658ex; height:3.676ex;"/></span>, for instance, is <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {7}{3}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>7</mn>
<mn>3</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {7}{3}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {7}{3}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8ec67c58a7efd85999ea24c8623927cfbba6e682" style="vertical-align: -1.338ex; width:1.658ex; height:3.843ex;"/></span>. The product of a fraction and its reciprocal is 1, hence the reciprocal is the <a href="/wiki/Multiplicative_inverse" title="Multiplicative inverse">multiplicative inverse</a> of a fraction. The reciprocal of a proper fraction is improper, and the reciprocal of an improper fraction not equal to 1 (that is, numerator and denominator are not equal) is a proper fraction.
</p><p>When the numerator and denominator of a fraction are equal (for example, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {7}{7}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>7</mn>
<mn>7</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {7}{7}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {7}{7}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/04c1068bab9bed21fb6ce73873a2bfffcc6cd24c" style="vertical-align: -1.338ex; width:1.658ex; height:3.843ex;"/></span>), its value is 1, and the fraction therefore is improper. Its reciprocal is identical and hence also equal to 1 and improper.
</p><p>Any integer can be written as a fraction with the number one as denominator. For example, 17 can be written as <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {17}{1}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>17</mn>
<mn>1</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {17}{1}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {17}{1}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/70103bc17f4ed61fafb19975bd74f4f6e422f6e4" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:2.48ex; height:3.676ex;"/></span>, where 1 is sometimes referred to as the <i>invisible denominator</i>. Therefore, every fraction or integer, except for zero, has a reciprocal. For example. the reciprocal of 17 is <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{17}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>17</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{17}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {1}{17}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/dd0bf5da67efba01a4bbd1c80988e7c84a2dd9ab" style="vertical-align: -1.338ex; width:2.48ex; height:3.676ex;"/></span>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ratios">Ratios</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fraction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Ratios">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Ratio" title="Ratio">ratio</a> is a relationship between two or more numbers that can be sometimes expressed as a fraction. Typically, a number of items are grouped and compared in a ratio, specifying numerically the relationship between each group. Ratios are expressed as "group 1 to group 2 ... to group <i>n</i>". For example, if a car lot had 12 vehicles, of which
</p>
<ul><li>2 are white,</li>
<li>6 are red, and</li>
<li>4 are yellow,</li></ul>
<p>then the ratio of red to white to yellow cars is 6 to 2 to 4. The ratio of yellow cars to white cars is 4 to 2 and may be expressed as 4:2 or 2:1.
</p><p>A ratio is often converted to a fraction when it is expressed as a ratio to the whole. In the above example, the ratio of yellow cars to all the cars on the lot is 4:12 or 1:3. We can convert these ratios to a fraction, and say that <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">4</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">12</span></span> of the cars or <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">3</span></span> of the cars in the lot are yellow. Therefore, if a person randomly chose one car on the lot, then there is a one in three chance or <a href="/wiki/Probability" title="Probability">probability</a> that it would be yellow.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decimal_fractions_and_percentages">Decimal fractions and percentages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fraction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Decimal fractions and percentages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A <b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decimal_fraction" title="Decimal fraction">decimal fraction</a></b> is a fraction whose denominator is not given explicitly, but is understood to be an integer power of ten. Decimal fractions are commonly expressed using decimal notation in which the implied denominator is determined by the number of <a href="/wiki/Numerical_digit" title="Numerical digit">digits</a> to the right of a <a href="/wiki/Decimal_separator" title="Decimal separator">decimal separator</a>, the appearance of which (e.g., a period, an <a href="/wiki/Interpunct#In_mathematics_and_science" title="Interpunct">interpunct</a> (·), a comma) depends on the locale (for examples, see <a href="/wiki/Decimal_separator#Hindu–Arabic_numeral_system" title="Decimal separator">decimal separator</a>). Thus, for 0.75 the numerator is 75 and the implied denominator is 10 to the second power, namely, 100, because there are two digits to the right of the decimal separator. In decimal numbers greater than 1 (such as 3.75), the <a href="/wiki/Fractional_part" title="Fractional part">fractional part</a> of the number is expressed by the digits to the right of the decimal (with a value of 0.75 in this case). 3.75 can be written either as an improper fraction, 375/100, or as a mixed number, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle 3{\tfrac {75}{100}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mn>3</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>75</mn>
<mn>100</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle 3{\tfrac {75}{100}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="3{\tfrac {75}{100}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ddb8c4ce78a55297404acafd5f71e7155c8d139c" style="vertical-align: -1.338ex; width:4.465ex; height:3.843ex;"/></span>.
</p><p>Decimal fractions can also be expressed using <a href="/wiki/Scientific_notation" title="Scientific notation">scientific notation</a> with negative exponents, such as <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6993602299999999999♠"></span>6.023<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−7</sup></span>, which represents 0.0000006023. The <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6993100000000000000♠"></span>10<sup>−7</sup></span> represents a denominator of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7007100000000000000♠"></span>10<sup>7</sup></span>. Dividing by <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7007100000000000000♠"></span>10<sup>7</sup></span> moves the decimal point 7 places to the left.
</p><p>Decimal fractions with infinitely many digits to the right of the decimal separator represent an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infinite_series" title="Infinite series">infinite series</a>. For example, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">3</span></span> = 0.333... represents the infinite series 3/10 + 3/100 + 3/1000 + ....
</p><p>Another kind of fraction is the <a href="/wiki/Percentage" title="Percentage">percentage</a> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">per centum</i>, meaning "per hundred", represented by the symbol %), in which the implied denominator is always 100. Thus, 51% means 51/100. Percentages greater than 100 or less than zero are treated in the same way, e.g. 311% equals 311/100, and −27% equals −27/100.
</p><p>The related concept of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Permille" title="Permille">permille</a></i> or <i>parts per thousand</i> (ppt) has an implied denominator of 1000, while the more general <a href="/wiki/Parts-per_notation" title="Parts-per notation">parts-per notation</a>, as in 75 <i>parts per million</i> (ppm), means that the proportion is 75/1,000,000.
</p><p>Whether common fractions or decimal fractions are used is often a matter of taste and context. Common fractions are used most often when the denominator is relatively small. By <a href="/wiki/Mental_calculation" title="Mental calculation">mental calculation</a>, it is easier to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multiply" title="Multiply">multiply</a> 16 by 3/16 than to do the same calculation using the fraction's decimal equivalent (0.1875). And it is more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accurate" title="Accurate">accurate</a> to multiply 15 by 1/3, for example, than it is to multiply 15 by any decimal approximation of one third. Monetary values are commonly expressed as decimal fractions with denominator 100, i.e., with two decimals, for example $3.75. However, as noted above, in pre-decimal British currency, shillings and pence were often given the form (but not the meaning) of a fraction, as, for example, "3/6" (read "three and six") meaning 3 shillings and 6 pence, and having no relationship to the fraction 3/6.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mixed_numbers"><span class="anchor" id="Mixed_numbers"></span>Mixed numbers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fraction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Mixed numbers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A <b>mixed numeral</b> (also called a <i>mixed fraction</i> or <i>mixed number</i>) is a traditional denotation of the sum of a non-zero integer and a proper fraction (having the same sign). It is used primarily in measurement: <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle 2{\tfrac {3}{16}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mn>2</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>3</mn>
<mn>16</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle 2{\tfrac {3}{16}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle 2{\tfrac {3}{16}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/406a97756a732889a0447f089d003fe0302cc9a7" style="vertical-align: -1.338ex; width:3.643ex; height:3.676ex;"/></span>inches, for example. Scientific measurements almost invariably use decimal notation rather than mixed numbers. The sum can be implied without the use of a visible operator such as the appropriate "+". For example, in referring to two entire cakes and three quarters of another cake, the numerals denoting the integer part and the fractional part of the cakes can be written next to each other as <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle 2{\tfrac {3}{4}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p><p>This tradition is, formally, in conflict with the notation in algebra where adjacent symbols, without an explicit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infix_operator" title="Infix operator">infix operator</a>, denote a product. In the expression <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle 2x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>

If <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">x</span> is replaced by, for example, the fraction <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {3}{4}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mn>4</mn>
</mfrac>

</p><p>When multiplication is intended, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle 2{\tfrac {b}{c}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p>

</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \quad 2\left({\frac {b}{c}}\right),\;\ldots }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \quad 2\left({\frac {b}{c}}\right),\;\ldots }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a5d14405f195fb4010d74b21dbbdf1c72135acff" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:13.926ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>An improper fraction can be converted to a mixed number as follows:
</p>
<ol><li>Using <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_division" title="Euclidean division">Euclidean division</a> (division with remainder), divide the numerator by the denominator. In the example, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {11}{4}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>11</mn>
<mn>4</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {11}{4}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {11}{4}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c70afe66f979258d384bcca02e1c2707191e8ceb" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:2.48ex; height:3.509ex;"/></span>, divide 11 by 4. 11 ÷ 4 = 2 remainder 3.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Quotient" title="Quotient">quotient</a> (without the remainder) becomes the whole number part of the mixed number. The remainder becomes the numerator of the fractional part. In the example, 2 is the whole number part and 3 is the numerator of the fractional part.</li>
<li>The new denominator is the same as the denominator of the improper fraction. In the example, it is 4. Thus, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {11}{4}}=2{\tfrac {3}{4}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>11</mn>
<mn>4</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>2</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>3</mn>
<mn>4</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {11}{4}}=2{\tfrac {3}{4}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {11}{4}}=2{\tfrac {3}{4}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/13b53160c1e31b631e45a61e244abc387e1b3265" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:8.399ex; height:3.509ex;"/></span>.</li></ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_notions">Historical notions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fraction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Historical notions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Egyptian_fraction">Egyptian fraction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fraction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Egyptian fraction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_fraction" title="Egyptian fraction">Egyptian fraction</a> is the sum of distinct positive unit fractions, for example <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{2}}+{\tfrac {1}{3}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<mo>+</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>3</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{2}}+{\tfrac {1}{3}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {1}{2}}+{\tfrac {1}{3}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/69c57825b3992ab0d1bddf17c6c68baa9dc13104" style="vertical-align: -1.338ex; width:6.157ex; height:3.676ex;"/></span>. This definition derives from the fact that the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egyptians</a> expressed all fractions except <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{2}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{2}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {1}{2}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/edef8290613648790a8ac1a95c2fb7c3972aea2f" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:1.658ex; height:3.509ex;"/></span>, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {2}{3}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mn>2</mn>
<mn>3</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {2}{3}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {2}{3}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/571a6ce6d697175e9e5e723b8c40eaa7efcfeaca" style="vertical-align: -1.338ex; width:1.658ex; height:3.676ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {3}{4}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>3</mn>
<mn>4</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {3}{4}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {3}{4}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8ec6051ef87eb0dafdaeaacd61f340052fcbf2bf" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:1.658ex; height:3.509ex;"/></span> in this manner. Every positive rational number can be expanded as an Egyptian fraction. For example, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {5}{7}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mn>21</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{2}}+{\tfrac {1}{6}}+{\tfrac {1}{21}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {1}{2}}+{\tfrac {1}{6}}+{\tfrac {1}{21}}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/027592e80dcfcc7338bea77451f9ac8132d2500e" style="vertical-align: -1.338ex; width:12.124ex; height:3.676ex;"/></span> Any positive rational number can be written as a sum of unit fractions in infinitely many ways. Two ways to write <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {13}{17}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Complex_and_compound_fractions">Complex and compound fractions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fraction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Complex and compound fractions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complex_numbers" title="Complex numbers">Complex numbers</a>.</div>
<p>In a <b>complex fraction</b>, either the numerator, or the denominator, or both, is a fraction or a mixed number,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trotter_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trotter-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barlow_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barlow-20">[19]</a></sup> corresponding to division of fractions. For example, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\frac {\tfrac {1}{2}}{\tfrac {1}{3}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>3</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {\tfrac {1}{2}}{\tfrac {1}{3}}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\frac {\tfrac {1}{2}}{\tfrac {1}{3}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8c016f364873ed0df2130302276eb33e82aaf93a" style="vertical-align: -3.505ex; width:2.494ex; height:8.009ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\frac {12{\tfrac {3}{4}}}{26}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<p>If, in a complex fraction, there is no unique way to tell which fraction lines takes precedence, then this expression is improperly formed, because of ambiguity. So 5/10/20/40 is not a valid mathematical expression, because of multiple possible interpretations, e.g. as
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle 5/(10/(20/40))={\frac {5}{10/{\tfrac {20}{40}}}}={\frac {1}{4}}\quad }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mn>10</mn>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \quad (5/10)/(20/40)={\frac {\tfrac {5}{10}}{\tfrac {20}{40}}}=1}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/40b7a6f9d8fd4be2849c62262fcebcbad5a8f851" style="vertical-align: -3.505ex; width:28.241ex; height:8.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>A <b>compound fraction</b> is a fraction of a fraction, or any number of fractions connected with the word <i>of</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trotter_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Trotter-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barlow_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barlow-20">[19]</a></sup> corresponding to multiplication of fractions. To reduce a compound fraction to a simple fraction, just carry out the multiplication (see the section on <a href="#Multiplication">multiplication</a>). For example, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {3}{4}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>3</mn>
<mn>4</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {3}{4}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {3}{4}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8ec6051ef87eb0dafdaeaacd61f340052fcbf2bf" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:1.658ex; height:3.509ex;"/></span> of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {5}{7}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>5</mn>
<mn>7</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {5}{7}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {5}{7}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a73779cafebb46314575d513d3c8d23ca2e2f150" style="vertical-align: -1.338ex; width:1.658ex; height:3.676ex;"/></span> is a compound fraction, corresponding to <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {3}{4}}\times {\tfrac {5}{7}}={\tfrac {15}{28}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>3</mn>
<mn>4</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<mo>×<!-- × --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>5</mn>
<mn>7</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>15</mn>
<mn>28</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {3}{4}}\times {\tfrac {5}{7}}={\tfrac {15}{28}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {3}{4}}\times {\tfrac {5}{7}}={\tfrac {15}{28}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87dc90452902aa17eb3f89682ceccc0bb1345a46" style="vertical-align: -1.338ex; width:11.735ex; height:3.676ex;"/></span>. The terms compound fraction and complex fraction are closely related and sometimes one is used as a synonym for the other. (For example, the compound fraction <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {3}{4}}\times {\tfrac {5}{7}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>3</mn>
<mn>4</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<mo>×<!-- × --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>5</mn>
<mn>7</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {3}{4}}\times {\tfrac {5}{7}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {3}{4}}\times {\tfrac {5}{7}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2fd409db0f2718ae21b277b86759372fee37d450" style="vertical-align: -1.338ex; width:6.157ex; height:3.676ex;"/></span> is equivalent to the complex fraction <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {3/4}{7/5}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mn>3</mn>

</mrow>
<mn>4</mn>
</mrow>
<mrow>
<mn>7</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mn>5</mn>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {3/4}{7/5}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {3/4}{7/5}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/19980cc8c935fddf56467d78d7af8ef2a118a985" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:3.302ex; height:4.843ex;"/></span>.)
</p><p>Nevertheless, "complex fraction" and "compound fraction" may both be considered outdated<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> and now used in no well-defined manner, partly even taken synonymously for each other<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> or for mixed numerals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> They have lost their meaning as technical terms and the attributes "complex" and "compound" tend to be used in their every day meaning of "consisting of parts".
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Arithmetic_with_fractions">Arithmetic with fractions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fraction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Arithmetic with fractions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Like whole numbers, fractions obey the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commutative" title="Commutative">commutative</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Associative" title="Associative">associative</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Distributive_property" title="Distributive property">distributive</a> laws, and the rule against <a href="/wiki/Division_by_zero" title="Division by zero">division by zero</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Equivalent_fractions">Equivalent fractions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fraction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Equivalent fractions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Multiplying the numerator and denominator of a fraction by the same (non-zero) number results in a fraction that is equivalent to the original fraction. This is true because for any non-zero number <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle n}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>n</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle n}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="n" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.395ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span>, the fraction <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {n}{n}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mi>n</mi>
<mi>n</mi>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {n}{n}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {n}{n}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f3674532e6aafc9ab6f559abd113aa2d03ac7a98" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:1.822ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span> equals <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle 1}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mn>1</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle 1}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="1" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/92d98b82a3778f043108d4e20960a9193df57cbf" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.162ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>. Therefore, multiplying by <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {n}{n}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mi>n</mi>
<mi>n</mi>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {n}{n}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {n}{n}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f3674532e6aafc9ab6f559abd113aa2d03ac7a98" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:1.822ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span> is the same as multiplying by one, and any number multiplied by one has the same value as the original number. By way of an example, start with the fraction <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{2}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{2}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {1}{2}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/edef8290613648790a8ac1a95c2fb7c3972aea2f" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:1.658ex; height:3.509ex;"/></span>. When the numerator and denominator are both multiplied by 2, the result is <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {2}{4}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>2</mn>
<mn>4</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {2}{4}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {2}{4}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e7ae6a7bf3fa207ef6bdbabc22404a3d0abb6c47" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:1.658ex; height:3.509ex;"/></span>, which has the same value (0.5) as <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{2}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{2}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {1}{2}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/edef8290613648790a8ac1a95c2fb7c3972aea2f" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:1.658ex; height:3.509ex;"/></span>. To picture this visually, imagine cutting a cake into four pieces; two of the pieces together (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {2}{4}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>2</mn>
<mn>4</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tfrac {2}{4}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\tfrac {2}{4}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e7ae6a7bf3fa207ef6bdbabc22404a3d0abb6c47" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:1.658ex; height:3.509ex;"/></span>) make up half the cake (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{2}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
